[2023-08-01 04:06:37] 614946962139250711: If you've use regular bark you can use the same strategy that people use to make voices. Your first prompt is not the song you actually want to make, your first prompt is just lyrics/text that has very strong associations with a genre of music that you want. Try a few times, once you find a good one, continue from that with your actual lyrics. It's still a lot of hit or miss but that's how I've made my favorite things (like the jazz Gangnam style, for example). 

***Method One***
1.** Style-Bait Prompt**, often with very *generic* or cliche lyrics. The goal isn't good lyrics, it's to find words strongly associated with a particular musical style.
2. Try a few times to find the right music vibe, potentially.
3. From the sample that sounds right, continue it with the lyrics you actually want.

Sometimes continuing doesn't produce a great output, especially if the first clip has an awkward ending. Sometimes trying to switch genre entirely causes a bad output. But sometimes it's AMAZING.

***Method Two***
**Same pattern, but in a single generation.** If you keep getting the same genre of music with your lyrics, and you don't want to continue, try adding a few lines from a classic Christmas song right in front of your lyrics.  (Christmas song is just an example of lyrics that have a strong association with a style of music, you can whatever you want. `Zip-a-dee-doo-dah, zip-a-dee-ay
My, oh, my, what a wonderful day`  works well)
```
Jingle bells, jingle bells
Jingle all the way
Oh, what fun it is to ride
In a one-horse open sleigh, hey!
```
Just 2 lines is probably enough, if you want to preserve the most space for your own lyrics. You'll need to edit the clip afterword of course.

[2023-08-01 06:43:54] 614946962139250711: 1. Type `/sing`, then push Enter. Sometimes I have to push Enter twice, not sure why. Maybe when I hit Enter too quickly after typing `/sing`. Actually I seem to always need to hit Enter twice, but this may not be universal.
2. There is model textbox popup. On my screen it is fairly small. A square of 3 or 4 inches in the very center of the screen. It is titled "Sing" and says "ENTER YOUR OWN LYRICS" before the text field where you put your lyrics. There is a second text field, text on screen text before is "...OR HAVE CHATGPT MAKE LYRICS ABOUT:" 
3. On my desktop computer the text cursor is already in the lyrics field, and I can just start typing lyrics. Or paste them.
4. At the bottom right of the modal popup are two buttons, "Cancel" and "Submit" 
5. If you are typing in the lyrics field, then hit TAB three times, the Submit button will be highlighted and you can push Enter.

[2023-08-01 08:54:54] 82995455746637824: When trying to influence the AI to give proper genres, do you have to format the tags in any special way? The AI started singing the tags I pasted, so I'm not super sure if I'm doing something wrong perhaps?
[2023-08-01 08:58:34] 614946962139250711: Influencing with tags is extremely hit or miss, in general. It can work but you really have to get lucky. Usually parenthesis (), and keep it simple. I saw somebody use `(Chorus - Creepy and Scary)` and that surprisingly sort of worked. A stronger influence is just to use an entire throwaway set of lyrics as the first generation, the first sample, but lyrics that are very strongly tropey or cliché or very well known songs that really exist and have many covers, so they model connects those words to a specific music genre. Then to use your real lyrics to 'continue' (it's an arrow button on the output in Discord) the sample.
[2023-08-01 09:05:23] 614946962139250711: You can try anything though. I got some very amusing results with court transcripts, literally this prompt. Linebreaks matter a lot, so try different variants of those too. 
```
THE COURT: This is the matter of the State versus John Doe.
MS. JANE: Yes. Mr. Doe is present in court and we're ready for motions.
THE COURT: Very well. Call your first witness.
MS. SMITH: The State calls Officer Peter Paul.
MS. SMITH: Could you please state your name for the Record?

A. Officer Peter Paul.
Q. Officer, how are you employed?
A. Police officer.
Q. Do you see Mr. Doe in court today?
A. Yes, right there at the desk in the orange jumpsuit.
```
You can also try capital letters, no :, one the line before:
```

WOTAN
Now bridle thy horse, warrior maid;
soon will blaze furious strife.
Brünnhilde, haste to the fray
to shield the Wälsung in fight!
There let Hunding go where he belongs;

LUKE
He told me enough.
He told me you killed him.

VADER
No.
I am your father.
```

[2023-08-01 15:00:53] 1069853697221337138: shorter songs (4-8 lines or less) tend to be more variable in terms of style
[2023-08-01 15:01:11] 614946962139250711: "how do you get this to have actual music with the lyrics, instead of just rap?"
```
(Verse)
Listen close, seize the key,
To unlock your Bark song's spree.
Commence with text, cliched and bright,
A style bait to set your music's sight.

(Chorus)
Hold that sound, make it last,
As it echoes the music's vast past.
First prompt, trite but might,
Then continue your song, breaking light.
From immense data, the AI gleaned,
Matching text to music, as if predestined.
```
It works best if you use an entire song as the style prompt, and then continue. 

But you can do a version of this pattern even in a single generation. If you keep getting the same genre of music with your lyrics, and you don't want to continue, try adding a few lines from a classic Christmas song in front of your lyrics. 
```
Jingle bells, jingle bells
Jingle all the way
Oh, what fun it is to ride
In a one-horse open sleigh, hey!
```
Just 2 lines is probably enough, if you want to preserve the most space for your own lyrics. (Christmas is just an example, any lyrics that have a very song genre connection will do.)

[2023-08-01 22:57:38] 936714328596430848: Ok...I'm new and I'm not the most tech savvy person in the world so could someone explain to me what all this is (layman's terms)
[2023-08-01 22:57:58] 1069853697221337138: we should probably clarify the rules (thats a bit confusing), meant to be more for the text chat
[2023-08-01 22:58:23] 936714328596430848: Wym
[2023-08-01 22:58:41] 1069853697221337138: sorry that was in response to a comment above
[2023-08-01 22:58:50] 936714328596430848: Ah
[2023-08-01 22:58:52] 142746339321577472: LLMs are trained on massive datasets of text data. This allows them to learn the statistical patterns and relationships between words and sentences.

The text data is broken down into smaller units called tokens. Tokens can be individual words, subword units, or even characters. During training, the LLM learns to predict the next token based on the previous tokens.

To generate text, the LLM is given a prompt as the starting tokens. It then predicts the next most likely token, and keeps generating more tokens to complete the text.

Sometimes, the LLM can generate text that seems coherent but is factually incorrect or hallucinated. This can happen because the LLM is only modeling the statistical patterns in the training data, not real world knowledge.

Hallucinations occur when the generated text wanders away from information present in the prompt and training data, into fiction. For example, if prompted to generate text about a made-up country, the LLM might generate plausible sounding but false details about that country's history or geography.

The risk of hallucinations depends on the prompt and how much the LLM has to generate text beyond what was in the prompt or training data. Hallucinations can be reduced by grounding the LLM in factual knowledge sources. But no model is perfect, so a degree of caution is warranted when relying on LLM generated text.

In summary, LLMs learn text patterns from data, generate text token-by-token, and can sometimes hallucinate fictional details when prompted to continue generating beyond their actual knowledge.

[2023-08-02 21:45:37] 614946962139250711: So there's two main strategies. 

1. Use one whole song generation (up to a 30s clip) that is just using 'throwaway' lyrifcs that are "style bait" to get the model to give you the type of you music you want. Then you click the arrow to continue the song, and use the lyrics you actually want.
2. The same idea, but in one prompt. Stick the first few lines of "Jingle Bells" in your lyrics, and the your actual lyrics. You'll have to edit the audio, but it will steer the output.

[2023-08-02 23:06:21] 614946962139250711: If you're using ChatGPT yourself I find with both lyrics, and creative writing, you almost have to be absurdly and comically literal in your instructions to be more creative. Pretend you're talking to Data from Star Trek. Like you would have to tell Data  "Use contractions, sometimes use `don't` instead of `do not` right? 

With ChatGPT it's the same. I literally put this instruction in my prompts `In English, subject-verb-object word order is the standard and correct grammar pattern. But in creative writing this rule is broken, sometimes quite often. Be sure to break this rule at least 1 time every 10 sentences.` and so on. It's not anything like would have to explain to a human, but for anything creative you have to be comically blunt with ChatGPT. It's so biased towards one particualar style or writing otherwise.

Funnily enough I ended up learning some things about English grammar just because I was trying to explain to ChatGPT why it's creative output was so bad, but I didn't have the technical vocabulary to know how to explain. Stress timing and rhythms in English is another one.
[2023-08-02 23:14:23] 614946962139250711: Another basic workflow is ask it one time, let it generate, then prompt again asking it to make it much less generic. 

Interestingly if you try to one shot this it doesn't work. Like if you say in one single prompt: "After you finish writing. Stop and think, criticize that writing, then try again and write a less generic one" that doesn't work nearly as well as having it generate the first idea, then you prompting it again. Even if you use the exact same words in your requests. Sometimes it works a little bit, but much much worse overall.
[2023-08-02 23:17:38] 614946962139250711: The TLDR is try to figure out how to explain the most literal, technical, and blunt ways possible, why the ChatGPT sample is bad. This is not always easy to figure out, but you can probably come up with some things at least. Then in very explicit terms request it not to do those things in the generation. You will eventually build up a long list of rules and instructions in your creative prompts and the output will slowly improve.
[2023-08-02 23:26:53] 614946962139250711: For longer pieces, try using multiple context windows, and having each act like it's a "writer's room" and passing the document back and forth. Ask it to write notes to "the other writers" that explain it's rational, and create a dialog. Then pass the responses back and forth between the two chatGPT contexts. Uses up lots of tokens but creative writing gets way better. And it's baking in long-term coherency, beause you treak it like a TV script that was being worked on by 3 or 4 differnet writers who have to stay coherent with each other's edits

[2023-08-03 01:25:24] 614946962139250711: ***Q: How can I control the genre of music with the Chirp AI?*** 

***A: Influence genre through lyrics***

Direct control of the music genre in Chirp isn't available (yet, coming in Chirp v2!) Your prompt with lyrics is your only input. This doesn't mean you don't have any influence. Like the Bark voice model, Chirp tends to match the style of music to the lyrics. If you use words common in death metal, Chirp tends to create death metal.

In Bark TTS, if you want a calm, soothing voice, you might prompt `Listen to my soothing, relaxing voice...`. If Chirp keeps generating the same genre of music with your lyrics then ***change your lyrics*** to those associated with a different music genre.

***Q: How can I control the music genre and still use the lyrics I actually want?***

***A: Two methods: 1️⃣ Reformat your lyrics, or 2️⃣ Use Style-Bait Lyrics***
[2023-08-03 01:25:36] 614946962139250711: ***1️⃣ : Reformat Your Lyrics***

1️⃣  **Vary Prompt Length:**

Word choice isn’t the only factor that makes some lyrics resemble certain music genres. *Longer *Chirp prompts tend to produce rap or hip-hop, presumably because those genres tend to have a lot of words in a short span of time, so longer lyrics look a bit like rap lyrics. So try *longer * prompts if you want rap/hip-hop, and *shorter * prompt if you want other genres.

1️⃣  **Vary Prompt Line Breaks:**

Lyrics formatted like this:
```
These lyrics
will sound
different than
the others
```
will sound different than these:
```
These lyrics will sound
different than the others
```
And this can influence the genre a bit as well.

1️⃣ **Try Prompt Tags:**

Tags are not intentional features - these are discovered capabilities. So they are very hit or miss. Try stuff and see what works, people are discovering new ones every day. For punctuation both `()` and `[]` can work. This a sampling of tags, not close to an exhaustive list, but you can get the idea:

*Song Structure - Usually Works*
```
(intro)
[verse 1]
(chorus)
[bridge]
```
*Genre Guidance - Sometimes Works*
```
(pop) or (dance) etc
maybe (verse 1: dance)
(instrumental)
[solo]
```
*Specific Names - Rarely Work (but you never know…)*
```
[Verse 1: Tyler, The Creator]
```
[2023-08-03 01:25:45] 614946962139250711: *** 2️⃣: Use Throwaway Lyrics as "Style-Bait" then ⏩ Continue With Real Lyrics***

This is the main way you can influence the music genre without chaning your lyrics. Your initial lyrics don't need to be what you actually want. Your first prompt can just be lyrics with strong genre associations - a pure style-bait prompt. 

1. Think about what lyrics could get Chirp to generate the genre you want. The goal isn't good lyrics - you actually want the very generic, cliché lyrics because those have the strongest genre connections. Well-known real songs with many covers can work too.
2. Generate multiple samples, test different prompts until you find your music style.
3. Once you've got a clip with that style, **continue **⏩  the clip with your actual lyrics, using the ⏩ button. The original sample can be 30s long, but a continued sample is currently limited to 20s. But you can continue multiple times: click the ❤️ button on the final clip, and download the full audio with all parts combined.
4. If the continuation didn't work well (sometimes it just doesn't), go back to step 1, try again. It may take a few tries but when it works, it is a real delight. (This is how I made my favorites like the jazz Gangnam style `https://suno-ai.notion.site/Chirp-Examples-f05351485da74d769d6183220a6e5da7#f78f8bf2b83e4975b2d86cd7257aba1c`

2️⃣ ***Method Two: Easy Mode: Continue ⏩ someone else's song***

All posts on Discord are public. You can browse other people's songs, find one in the genre you want, and continue from there.

2️⃣ ***Method Two: One-Prompt Variant***

This method provides less influence over the genre than two separate Chirp generations, but it can still work. Use the style-bait lyrics and your lyrics in the same prompt, ~2-4 lines of bait.

```
Jingle bells, jingle bells
Jingle all the way
My Lyrics Go Here
After The Jingle Bells
```
A fun thing about this way is it is a bit like a genre knob. Want to turn up the Jingle Bells a notch? Add just  one more line of lyrics.

[2023-08-03 19:56:00] 238071088666509312: ***Q: How can I control the genre of music with the Chirp AI?*** 

***A: Influence genre through lyrics***

Direct control of the music genre in Chirp isn't available. Your prompt with lyrics is your only input. This doesn't mean you don't have any influence. Like the Bark voice model, Chirp tends to match the style of music to the lyrics. If you use words common in death metal, Chirp tends to create death metal.

In Bark TTS, if you want a calm, soothing voice, you might prompt `Listen to my soothing, relaxing voice...`. If Chirp keeps generating the same genre of music with your lyrics then ***change your lyrics*** to those associated with a different music genre.

***Q: How can I control the music genre and still use the lyrics I actually want?***

***A: Two methods: 1️⃣ Reformat your lyrics, or 2️⃣ Use Style-Bait Lyrics***
[2023-08-03 19:56:54] 238071088666509312: ***1️⃣ : Reformat Your Lyrics***

1️⃣  **Vary Prompt Length:**

Word choice isn’t the only factor that makes some lyrics resemble certain music genres. *Longer *Chirp prompts tend to produce rap or hip-hop, presumably because those genres tend to have a lot of words in a short span of time, so longer lyrics look a bit like rap lyrics. So try *longer * prompts if you want rap/hip-hop, and *shorter * prompt if you want other genres.

1️⃣  **Vary Prompt Line Breaks:**

Lyrics formatted like this:
```
These lyrics
will sound
different than
the others
```
will sound different than these:
```
These lyrics will sound
different than the others
```
And this can influence the genre a bit as well.

1️⃣ **Try Prompt Tags:**

Tags are not intentional features - these are discovered capabilities. So they are very hit or miss. Try stuff and see what works, people are discovering new ones every day. For punctuation both `()` and `[]` can work. This a sampling of tags, not close to an exhaustive list, but you can get the idea:

*Song Structure - Usually Works*
```
(intro)
[verse 1]
(chorus)
[bridge]
```
*Genre Guidance - Sometimes Works*
```
(pop) or (dance) etc
maybe (verse 1: dance)
(instrumental)
[solo]
```
*Specific Names - Rarely Work (but you never know…)*
```
[Verse 1: Tyler, The Creator]
```

[2023-08-04 02:12:25] 348175347973619712: When using Bark via the Python API `generate_audio`, is there a way to determine if a prompt could not fit in the 13 second window? I'm guessing the model isn't "aware" when the prompt cannot fit, but I figure it's worth asking. 🙂
[2023-08-04 02:26:32] 614946962139250711: Not built in, you just have to feel it out. I like to aim for 25 ish words, divided by sentences. You can count sentences/words/characters. And it's a matter of preference too. Up to a point Bark will *try* to fit in all the prompt it can in the 13 second window, though some speakers are much better than others at it. So longer prompt = faster speech, generally.
[2023-08-04 02:33:13] 348175347973619712: Makes sense, thanks. I imagine Bark also tries to draw out shorter sentences to fill the full window? Besides counting words, do you recommend anything to keep the speech cadence from fluctuating too much when concatenating audio for longer form generations?
[2023-08-04 02:39:33] 614946962139250711: Yeah, you got it, Bark will tend to draw out shorter sentences with pauses and even adding filler words like "Um" or "So" etc. However you can usually just try again and get a sample that is genuinely short, it's still a tendency not a rule. I haven't found any simple rules other than trial and error, and lots of testing. Run 20 voices on 20 prompts. The ones that are most stable, set aside, and then look through the samples and pick a few of the samples to use as alternative versions. Then throw those into your new set of voices, and test again. (Using the save_as_prompt function so you always making new versions of voices, if you are on base repo.)
[2023-08-04 02:40:18] 614946962139250711: Tags like [laughs] tend to make a lot of voices unstable. Any usual text can, as well. Foreign langugae words.
[2023-08-04 02:44:01] 238071088666509312: I'm starting to figure out, if you want to do a song, start from the chorus, and work your way from there.
[2023-08-04 02:48:21] 348175347973619712: I'm using the suno-ai/bark repo from github, which I imagine is the base repo? I found `save_as_prompt` in there, but I don't see any documentation for it. If you don't mind continuing this awesome help a little longer, can you explain the npz files? I only thought of them as "speakers" but it looks like `save_as_prompt` generates these. What is the `full_generation` that gets saved - is that a single numpy audio array, or is that a series of many generations?
[2023-08-04 02:48:56] 348175347973619712: It sounds like your approach is more evolutionary than I was imagining is possible
[2023-08-04 02:57:14] 614946962139250711: In Bark, speakers are .npz files, .npz files are numpy arrays of Bark tokens (from 3 models), and Bark audio outputs... are also Bark tokens. So Bark Speakers, .npz files, and audio samples, are all the same thing in Bark. When you use a speaker, you are just loading those tokens into the context, the history, Bark is just continuing the audio from that context, which functionally acts like a voice. `full_generation` means you get back not just the decoded audio, but all three arrays of tokens. So you can then save that sample as a new .npz file. 

If you give Bark no voice, no .npz file, (history_prompt parameter) then bark makes a random voice. Then if you save hat sample as a new .npz file, you create a new voice. This same pattern works in a more 'evoluationary' workflow even if you start from a .npz file. If you have Bark use that .npz history_prompt, generate an audio clip, then save the full generation, that's a new version of the voice. Because of both your text prompt, and random variation, that audio sample may be a slightly different version of your voice. So if you use the new version, from the other sample, it's a voice variant.

A very clear cut example is speaking speed. Take an existing voice and ask it to speak a lot of words, maybe 20 samples. Choose an amount of words that it doesn't finish in most of the 20 samples. Then take the 2 or 3 where it DID it finish in time, and test those voice. Very often those voices will now speak faster than the original in general, so you can use that as a speaker variant. However it's a little tricky because Bark voices often drift, so you always risk the voice changing in these new versions. But it is just a matter of trying again, you can get samples where it doesn't drift. And you only need one good one.
[2023-08-04 03:05:46] 348175347973619712: A lot of things make more sense to me now. Pardon my ignorance here, but is the characteristic of Bark that it " is just continuing the audio from that context" because it is a generative pre-trained transformer? Sounds to me like how an LLM works to some degree, though I'm slightly out of my depth (I am a software engineer but very new to generative AI and just learning as a hobby).

Regardless, I think you just unlocked for me how I can get what I'd like to out of Bark, and I really appreciate it!

I was hoping I might use different voices for dialogue, and now I think I see how I might accomplish that without being limited to the npz files in the repo. Thanks again!
[2023-08-04 03:07:16] 856546017998929971: yup it's basically an llm for audio
[2023-08-04 03:14:27] 614946962139250711: Yeah your mental model of Bark will make more sense if you think about it in llm terms absolutely.
[2023-08-04 03:15:21] 348175347973619712: Oh sorry, I meant to ask one last thing. Based on my new understanding of the prompt history and npz files, can this also be used in long generations to do better than simply concatenating different generations like in the IPython notebook example? It sounds like reusing the last state might generate more fluid audio over sequential prompts.
[2023-08-04 03:16:54] 614946962139250711: It can, but it's not that simple. You'll get accumulated errors, feedback, and voice drifting. You can mix and match partial history + partial last sample, carefully, but it's a bit fiddly.
[2023-08-04 03:17:54] 614946962139250711: There are other things that work, like I was trying some really wild ideas that did work but 2x or 4x generation time, and they didn't sound that much better than simply reusing the same .npz.
[2023-08-04 03:19:39] 614946962139250711: However, a really simple upgrade is to rotate through a few different .npz variants. For example a 'start of new paragraph' .npz version, and a 'picking up mid paragraph' version. Not any technical way of making these, just trying variants of voice and listening, and noticing if some work better to continue an existing flow, and others to start a new section. Then if you use both the overall audio is a little more rich.
[2023-08-04 03:20:20] 614946962139250711: Audiobooks are still tricky with the small context, there's just a hard problem there. You can say a sentence at the top of the page, that should have a strong influence on how a sentence sounds at the bottom of the page, even though that's 90 seconds later in the audio, long long out of context. Both better and worse when using dialogs with characters, different problems with long term audio. It still sounds good if you manually adjust the samples.
[2023-08-04 03:23:16] 348175347973619712: I'm mostly just playing, so it doesn't have to be perfect. These are great tips. Thanks!
[2023-08-04 03:27:40] 614946962139250711: This advice won't be useful until you get in a bit deeper in the technical weeds, but just note it for later. Little trick for making semi-random clear Bark voices. Start with any other .npz file that is clear. Ideally with a speech cadence that is something like you want. But only pass the context, the history_prompt, to the first Bark model, the text/semantic one. Just forget about for the others. You'll get a lot of voices that sound quite different, with a similar-ish rhythm or vibe, and are typically fairly clear of background nosie.
[2023-08-04 03:48:21] 348175347973619712: Thanks, I'll be honest that I haven't fully wrapped my head around the three models yet. I hope to make some progress on that though. 🙂
[2023-08-04 04:02:37] 348175347973619712: Are semantic tokens human-intelligible or something that only makes sense in latent space? I can imagine they might encode pausing and intonation and not be human intelligible.
[2023-08-04 04:27:13] 614946962139250711: There are a *little* bit intelligible, but not really. There's 10,000 tokens. Pauses and silence might be a string of token ID '10' repeated, in a quiet spot, for example. Intonation and words I don't think so. And some of the tokens are more language like. The vast vast majority of the time if you snip a section of semantic tokens, the audio is only changed at that snip point. But once in awhile it changes the audio for the * rest of the clip*, like it's an instruction "start playing this sound" or more likely just the presence of that sequence is having a strong influence how the later tokens are rendered and the chance of having that sound. So it feels like a little on/off switch for a sound. TLDR Not really, but a little bit for the quiet parts.
[2023-08-04 04:31:40] 614946962139250711: I had a Bark voice that had a very high chance of switching voices to a new person, maybe 1 in 3 samples had the voice entirely switch after a few words. I randomly removed chunks, and there one segment of maybe 5 tokens, if I took it out, it never switched. The segment made no sound, was inaudible. I tried copy and pasting that sequence into another voice to see if would work as a 'stay on target' signal, and it didn't transfer over. The sequence only functioned like that in that one voice. Sometimes they seem almost intelligible but realistically, semantic tokens are probably always a mystery like that.

[2023-08-04 04:44:27] 614946962139250711: min_eos_p  is a parameter in generate_text_semantic, which is one of lower level functions called by the audio function you are calling. It effects the chance of the audio clip ending. For shorter text prompts, you often need to make it a bit lower, or Bark will talk past the prompt. Often just adding Ummm, but could even add new words.

[2023-08-04 04:53:55] 614946962139250711: I'll copy and paste this from what I messaged him, in case it's useful
[2023-08-04 04:54:00] 614946962139250711: Note: A blind user having trouble using `/chirp` said this: `So it turns out, the issue was that there is that little message that appears prompting me to accept ToS, but my screen reader doesn't alert me that that message appears in the chat, so I had to look through the bots replies to find it.`

1. Type `/chirp`, then push Enter, and Enter again. Alternatively type `/chirp` then type Space, then Enter.  
2. There is a modal textbox, a popup. On my screen it is fairly small. A square of 3 or 4 inches in the very center of the screen. It is titled "Sing" and says "ENTER YOUR OWN LYRICS" before the text field where you put your lyrics. There is a second text field, text on screen text before is "...OR HAVE CHATGPT MAKE LYRICS ABOUT:"
3. On my desktop computer the text cursor is already in the lyrics field, and I can just start typing lyrics. Or paste them if had copied some before.
4. At the bottom right of the modal popup are two buttons, "Cancel" and "Submit"
5. If you are typing in the lyrics field, then hit TAB three times, the Submit button will be highlighted and you can push Enter.

[2023-08-06 09:57:09] 1058476387033419888: is bark able to use an audio transcription as an input instead of a text input,
[2023-08-06 10:25:03] 614946962139250711: On an existing song clip you can click the ⏩1 or ⏩2 buttons (for left or right sample) to add 20 more seconds. Repeat as many times as you want. Then click the ❤️ button, and the bot will respond with a clip of all the song sections put together.
[2023-08-06 10:25:51] 614946962139250711: Isn't audio transcription text? If you mean a subtitle or some text format, then not in the base repo, but it would just be a matter of converting it.
[2023-08-06 10:27:18] 1058476387033419888: i mean using a digital audio sample of someone speaking as an imput
[2023-08-06 10:27:44] 1058476387033419888: speech to speech
[2023-08-06 10:31:21] 614946962139250711: Ah, okay. So you mean preserving the performance in the original audio file, the rhythm, emotion, everything. This isn't a feature of Bark in the main repo, and not a feature in its design. However, if you 'clone' a voice (also not a feature in the official Bark) and then shove the cloned voice tokens for just the 'text' model into the Bark generation, it can sometimes kind of work like this. Overall though if want voice to voice you probably be better off using an entirely separate model.

Voice to Voice is really cool in Bark if you don't care about accurately reproducing the speech though. If you don't mind things being completely wrong, sounding very strange, etc. It's a useful method for sound experimentation and making things that sound *interesting*, if not accurate. But for *practical* use, if you just want to accurately convert one vocal sample into another voice, an RVC based model is likely what you want. If you're trying to make music or art or something it's worth trying in Bark though.

***Text -> Bark -> Voice to Voice*** is a common workflow for high quality audio, but that's using text as input.
[2023-08-06 10:32:15] 614946962139250711: Even when it works in Bark it doesn't work for long, like a clip longer than 15 or maybe 30 ish seconds.

[2023-08-06 11:16:57] 536290585141575691: does anyone know if there is a way to add long pauses, like 10 seconds? I am creating a hypnosis script and want a pause between each 14 second spoken line.
[2023-08-06 11:41:12] 614946962139250711: You can add silence in the way the clips are put together, it's a feature in my Bark fork at least, "add silence" I think. But this can sound a bit unnatural. Other ways (including in the text prompt):

1. Use a naturally slow Bark speaker .npz file. 
2. Use shorter text prompts, in general.
3. Try adding '...' or '(pause)' like "I'm going to pause. ... I'm done pausing." - it will work with some speakers.

Here's a slower speaker. The text in that prompt will generate more slow speakers from Bark as well.
[2023-08-06 11:43:26] 536290585141575691: thank you! what speaker did you use for your file? @Jonathan Fly
[2023-08-06 11:43:42] 614946962139250711: That's a random voice, so I posted the speaker below the clip right there.
[2023-08-06 11:44:13] 614946962139250711: Not 100% sure it will work well (sometimes they just don't) but if use a random voice with that prompt, and save it as an .npz, you can get other slow speakers.
[2023-08-06 11:45:43] 614946962139250711: If you're using the Bark bot, you have to generate them like that. Use the words:
```
Listen to my soothing, relaxing voice. Breathe calmly in, and out. Slowly close your eyes. Continue to breathe at this slow pace. Feel the air expand your lungs with each in breath. 
``` 
In Bark, with a random voice, then save that voice in the bot. You may have to try a few times, but when you get a good one you can save on the bot too.

[2023-08-08 04:16:50] 681223652762845211: From my experience you get emotions based on the text, like a text that people usually say when they are angry is more likelly to sound angry. You can try to generate first with a text that fits the emotion you want using the output_full=True and use that history_prompt to generate the other text like:

history_prompt, audio_array = generate_audio(base_emotion_prompt, output_full=True)
audio_array = generate_audio(prompt, history_prompt=history_prompt)

[2023-08-08 12:00:00] 710574486692429975: what I found out so far in trying to steer Chirp, I hope this might be of help. Chirp does not always follow these notions, but they often enough work just fine.

^ indicates an upbeat. for example '^Go' would put the word  as upbeat, while 'Go' makes the word start on the regular rhythm.
(...) helps indicate Onomatopoeia, for example (Ugh!), (Aha) etc. (Still not sure if the exclamation mark makes a difference)
"-" between characters makes Chirp voice each character separately (I needed to put the - between quotes here, otherwise it would be transformed automatically to a bullet point). for example gre-e-et. please be aware that for letters that are pronounced differently individually than in a word context, inserting a hyphen will result in the same pronunciation as for individual letters. 

Does anyone have more suggestions for notions to try out? would be nice to help each other to find out on how to steer Chirp to the results we want.

[2023-08-09 02:48:50] 681223652762845211: You can use the output_full=True to return a history_prompt and save it using the save_as_prompt:


from bark import SAMPLE_RATE, generate_audio, preload_models, save_as_prompt
from scipy.io.wavfile import write as write_wav

preload_models()

prompt = "I have a silky smooth voice, and today I will tell you about the exercise regimen of the common sloth."

history_prompt, audio_array = generate_audio(prompt, output_full=True)
save_as_prompt("path/to/history_prompt.npz", history_prompt)
write_wav("path/to/audio.wav", SAMPLE_RATE, audio_array)


So next time you can load that history_prompt:


from bark import SAMPLE_RATE, generate_audio, preload_models
from scipy.io.wavfile import write as write_wav

preload_models()

prompt = "Hello, my name is Suno. And, uh — and I like pizza. [laughs] But I also have other interests such as playing tic tac toe."

history_prompt = "path/to/history_prompt.npz"

audio_array = generate_audio(prompt, history_prompt=history_prompt)
write_wav("path/to/audio_2.wav", SAMPLE_RATE, audio_array)

[2023-08-09 10:10:16] 1004329274481844244: i get this type of error what should i do ValueError: `do_sample` is set to `False`. However, temperature is set to 0.5 -- this flag is only used in sample-based generation modes. Set `do_sample=True` or unset temperature to continue. or how can i do it i used this script from transformers import AutoProcessor, BarkModel
import scipy 

processor = AutoProcessor.from_pretrained("suno/bark")
model = BarkModel.from_pretrained("suno/bark")

voice_preset = "v2/en_speaker_6"

inputs = processor("Hello, my dog is cute", voice_preset=voice_preset)

audio_array = model.generate(**inputs)
audio_array = audio_array.cpu().numpy().squeeze()

## Save the .wav file into the disk 

sample_rate = model.generation_config.sample_rate
scipy.io.wavfile.write("ff", rate=sample_rate, data=audio_array)

[2023-08-12 06:49:18] 1027362153855979684: Hi guys, so I've been experimenting with different musical genres to try to get as close a match to the way I wanted my written song lyrics to sound. I realize that "chirp" is still in it's early Alpha stage (pretty darn impressive for an Alpha) by the way. What I'd like to know is if anyone here within this channel has come up with a creative way to nudge or coax the AI chirp bot into using specific vocals, such as female vocals for a particular set genre of music. For example, I have used the following prompt: /chirp Make a song from lyrics. genre_Cyberpunk Synthwave with female vocals. I know that female vocals are possible, as I have experienced it once before with one of my song lyrics, but just trying to get "chirp" to stick to using one type of sung vocal is tricky, because it is so random. Any ideas on what I could possibly try? Has anyone come up with an effective prompt to get "chirp" to stick to a specific type of sung vocal, instead of mostly just male sung vocals, and the possibility of being able to switch from male to female sung vocals? Or is everything just randomized between music genres and sung vocals?
[2023-08-12 06:54:16] 1027362153855979684: Hey @ZY I was wondering the very same thing, you were kind of ahead on me on that one. I think it's mostly randomized and just a matter of luck based on the type of song lyrics. I did notice though that with something like a rap song, and sometimes even soft rock or pop rock lyrics will sometimes produce female vocals if that helps any. As for a specific prompt, sorry couldn't really tell you. I've been experimenting myself to try to get chirp to sing in a specific vocal gender besides just male.
[2023-08-12 07:28:11] 456226577798135808: @admin Will there be Chirp 2.0. with custom durations?
[2023-08-12 07:29:36] 614946962139250711: I haven't tested gender influence but some general ideas here https://discord.com/channels/1069381916492562582/1069381916492562585/1136469889901670481 

The easiest method is to simply browse other people's clips, or just try a lot of very different prompts, not using any of lyrics you actually want. Eventually you'll hit on some female singers in the style you want. Then click the arrow button and continue, and now use your real lyrics. Sometimes continuing doesn't work, but it *can* work, either just trying again, or finding a different clip to continue from.

[2023-08-17 14:28:16] 614946962139250711: For Bark singing voices are rare enough that you probably need to not use a random voice, but a Bark voice that is already singing. [2023-08-24 01:17:27] 1054212796251906088: Hi  there can we create instrumentals????
[2023-08-24 01:22:44] 614946962139250711: It will be pretty easy in the next version. You can usually find a way to make it happen in the current version with creative prompts. Try `...` or `[instrumental]` etc
[2023-08-24 03:23:39] 582539387464843278: Are there any methods to force the model to do audio instead of speech? I keep getting voices reading the text of my prompt. Note: I’m not using “voice prompt” or anything
[2023-08-24 03:32:05] 614946962139250711: In `/Chirp` or in Bark itself? In Bark non speech is like pulling teeth but you can try adding the note emojis, using meta tags like `[music]`, and formatting the text to look more like music (typically removing periods and other sentence sending punctuation).
[2023-08-24 03:33:24] 614946962139250711: You could also do unconditional generation in Bark with basically nothing in your prompt, and sometimes that can get you some interesting non speech voices to starting building off of. (Set `allow_early_stop=False` or the audio samples tend to just end right away) One you do get a good non speech output in Bark, you can can then try saving that as a new 'voice' and continuing from there. But getting the first bunch of good non-speech voices may take awhile. However Bark is not a music or sound effect model like MusicGen or AudioGen. Between those two it can do MusicGen like stuff better, some cool singing and music, and it can still have the human vocal elements and complexity you won't find in MusicGen.

Sound effects do exist in Bark but the level of control and reliability is probably not worth it compared to other models. However if you're generating mostly speech you can sometimes put in a car horn or a [ding] in Bark and it works fine.
[2023-08-24 03:34:24] 614946962139250711: If you mean `/Chirp` then not sure what's going wrong, but formatting your song lyrics to look more like lyrics in the same way can help. And in Chirp (but not Bark) line breaks are very important.

[2023-08-24 15:38:19] 257596884464566278: In terms of Bark Infinity, is it possible to force a specific emotion in the prompt?
[2023-08-24 15:42:52] 614946962139250711: Not right now. Maybe some day. What you can do is try the low tech solution. Write a bunch of really emotion text prompts, and save the outputs as new voices. Sometimes you can get a version of the original voice that is like a emotion-modified variant.
[2023-08-24 15:44:08] 614946962139250711: The cool thing about Bark is that voices and audio samples are the same thing in bark. 

In Bark, speakers are .npz files, .npz files are numpy arrays of Bark tokens (from 3 models), and Bark audio outputs... are also Bark tokens. So Bark Speakers, .npz files, and audio samples, are all the same thing in Bark. When you use a speaker, you are just loading those tokens into the context, the history, Bark is just continuing the audio from that context, which functionally acts like a voice. full_generation means you get back not just the decoded audio, but all three arrays of tokens. So you can then save that sample as a new .npz file. 

If you give Bark no voice, no .npz file, (history_prompt parameter) then bark makes a random voice. Then if you save hat sample as a new .npz file, you create a new voice. This same pattern works in a more 'revolutionary' workflow even if you start from a .npz file. If you have Bark use that .npz history_prompt, generate an audio clip, then save the full generation, that's a new version of the voice. Because of both your text prompt, and random variation, that audio sample may be a slightly different version of your voice. So if you use the new version, from the other sample, it's a voice variant.

A very clear cut example is speaking speed. Take an existing voice and ask it to speak a lot of words, maybe 20 samples. Choose an amount of words that it doesn't finish in most of the 20 samples. Then take the 2 or 3 where it DID it finish in time, and test those voice. Very often those voices will now speak faster than the original in general, so you can use that as a speaker variant. However it's a little tricky because Bark voices often drift, so you always risk the voice changing in these new versions. But it is just a matter of trying again, you can get samples where it doesn't drift. And you only need one good one.
[2023-08-24 15:47:13] 614946962139250711: "does  the audio need to be generated specifically from bark and then saved?" Not absolutely, but it works much better if it was a real Bark audio output. 

The other way, to use fully arbitrary audio samples, is to use the 'voice cloning' community developed feature, not part of the official Bark. It wasn't trained on music and it uses an English speech based model (Hubert) as part of the way it works, so it doesn't work well for cloning music. HOWEVER it works great for 'making Bark do really strange things and give you sounds you can't get otherwise'
[2023-08-24 15:49:09] 614946962139250711: I cloned a pirate shanty song, and when I use that Bark voice... well. The voice literally yells and screams like this, about whatever prompt I give it. I don't know why or how but it's amazing. (Rants are repetitive and rhythmic, like music, so there is some connection to the original voice.) But basically for art and exploration you may actually really like trying to clone music and just getting surprised and confused. Embrace the POWER of Bark hallucinations.
[2023-08-24 15:52:10] 614946962139250711: As for the style transfer, if you dig into the code, you can try something like using one voices semantic tokens in the prompt for another. Or cloned music semantic tokens. This does not work 'well' in the sense of being an accurate style transfer, or a voice to voice model. However, as mentioned, stuff like this in Bark does work amazingly well at surprising you with bizarre and strange outputs.
[2023-08-24 15:57:46] 614946962139250711: Bark is quite good as making the audio feel human and expressive. You might not be able to get a perfect style transfer or get the sound you *want*, but you can get audio clips that have a crazy verisimilitude. Background chatter, a car engine, a baby crying, and muffled argument, and it just feels like you opened you window and listened. Even when it's grainy and distorted the whole clip has the feel of a scene, not an audio clip.

[2023-08-30 02:17:59] 614946962139250711: It's on the Github. Chirp is mostly English but more soon.
[2023-08-30 02:23:14] 614946962139250711: To make a voice slower or faster you only really need one generation, so even if it degrade it wouldn't be an issue. Just prompt the voice you like with some text that is typically read slowly, try to find text formatting that makes it slower (adding ... for example). Write up maybe 10 or so prompts. Try to find text with feels slow, so when you read it in your head, you can almost hear the pauses.

Then have the voice try each of the 10 lines few times. Go through the samples, you should have at least one that sounds fine in terms of sound quality, matches the original voice, and is slower and more like you want. Then try using this voice. There's still like a 50% chance that effect won't carryover when applied to any text generally, but if it doesn't, you can keep trying. It's not that much work, just some patience. And once you have a good one you can use it forever forward.
[2023-08-30 02:24:32] 614946962139250711: If that doesn't work even after 30 samples tried twice, send it to me and I'll slow it down
[2023-08-30 02:45:15] 504387519279005717: https://colab.research.google.com/github/camenduru/seamless-m4t-colab/blob/main/seamless_m4t_colab.ipynb
